[1] Chapter 1
[2] 1
[3] He arrived at the ferry terminal as the dusk was thickening.
[4] He could have arrived much earlier. In fact, it was his own deliberate procrastination.
[5] First, he had lunch with his friends at the "Red Wharf," chatting casually about anything and everything, exchanging gossip about their mutual acquaintances, all of which only meant that he was still hesitating inside to face the inevitable. His friends invited him to stay for tea, which he accepted. However, the time finally came, and he knew that he could not delay any longer.
[6] The hired car waited outside, and he bid farewell to his friends and drove along the congested seaside road for seven miles, turning inland and descending a wooded lane to reach the stone jetty by the river's edge.
[7] There was a large bell there, and the driver struck it with force, summoning the ferry across the river.
[8] "You don't need me to wait here, sir?"
[9] "No," said Arthur Calgarth, "I have called a car to pick me up in an hour on the other side – to take me to Dulmores."
[10] The driver took the fare and tip. He gazed into the darkened riverbank opposite and said, "The ferry is coming, sir."
[11] The driver backed out and said goodnight quietly, then drove up the hill. Arthur Calgarth was left alone on the jetty, with nothing but his thoughts and a slight unease about what lay ahead. The scenery was so bleak, he thought, feeling as if he were in the Scottish Lake District, cut off from the world. Yet there were hotels, shops, cocktail bars, and the bustling crowds of "Red Wharf" just a few miles away.
[12] He heard the gentle lapping of the water as the ferry approached the jetty. Arthur Calgarth descended the sloping ramp and boarded the boat when the ferryman had secured it with a hook. The ferryman was an old man, who gave Calgarth an odd impression of being inseparable from his boat, as if they were one and the same.
[13] As they pushed off from the shore, a cold breeze blew in from the sea, rustling the trees.
[14] "It's a chilly evening tonight," the ferryman said.
[15] Calgarth responded politely and agreed that it was colder than last night.
[16] He sensed, or thought he sensed, a curiosity in the ferryman's eyes. A stranger had arrived, a stranger who had come after the end of the tourist season. Moreover, this stranger had chosen an unusual time to cross the river—too late for a leisurely afternoon tea at the café on the other side of the dock. He had no luggage, so he was not staying overnight. (Calgary wondered himself why he had come so late. Was it because, in his subconscious, he had been trying to postpone this moment, to drag it out as long as possible?)
[17] He had crossed the Rubicon—the river...the river...His thoughts returned to another river—the Thames. He had been staring absently at it (just yesterday, wasn't it?) and then turned to look at the man across the table from him. There was something in those thoughtful eyes that he couldn't quite understand. Reserved, thinking about something, but not saying it...
[18] He supposed, he thought, that people had learned to keep their innermost thoughts hidden.
[19] It was only when he actually started doing it that he realized how awkward the whole thing was. He had to do it, had to do it—and then, after that, he had to—forget it!
[20] As he recalled yesterday's conversation, Calgary furrowed his brow. The pleasant, unruffled, and noncommittal voice had asked, "Are you sure you want to do this, Dr. Calgary?"
[21] He huffed, "What else can I do? You understand, don't you? You agree, don't you? I can't avoid this."
[22] But he didn't grasp the evasive look in those gray eyes, and his subsequent response confused him.
[23] "For a problem, you must consider it from all angles—from every perspective."
[24] "From a fair and just perspective, couldn't you just consider it from one angle?"
[25] The thought of this being a blatant suggestion to "cover up" the matter made him angry as he spoke.
[26] "To some extent, yes. But you also know that things aren't that simple. Or we could say... it's not just about fairness and justice?"
[27] "I don't agree. The family still needs to be considered."
[28] The other person quickly responded, "Yes... yes, that's right... I've taken them into account."
[29] This statement seemed absurd to Calgary, as if he had taken them into account –
[30] But the person continued speaking, still in a pleasant tone.
[31] "It entirely depends on you, Dr. Calgary. Of course, you do as you see fit."
[32] The boat stopped at the beach, and Calgary had made up his mind.
[33] The boatman spoke with a soft western accent, "Four pence for the fare, sir, or are you going back?"
[34] "No," Calgary said, "not going back." (How ominous that sounds!)
[35] He paid the fare and asked, "Do you know a place called Sunset Corner?"
[36] Suddenly, that curiosity could no longer be restrained. The old man's eyes glinted with intense interest.
[37] "Oh, certainly. It's right there, along the path to your right, just past the trees. You go up the hill, follow the path to the right, then take the new road through the residential area, and that's the one—right at the end."
[38] "Thank you."
[39] "You mean Sun Angle, sir? That's Mrs. Agle—"
[40] "Yes, yes. Sun Angle." Calgary cut him off, not wanting to discuss it further. "Sun Angle."
[41] The boatman's lips twisted slightly, and he managed a somewhat peculiar smile that made him suddenly seem like a cunning satyr from Roman mythology.
[42] "That's how she started calling the house, during the war. Of course, the house was just built then, brand new, but it didn't have a name. However, the place where the house was built - that tree-covered promontory - was actually called Snake's Head! But Snake's Head didn't suit her taste, it couldn't be her house's name. So she called it the Sunny Corner. Although we all still call it Snake's Head."
[43] Calgary abruptly thanked him and said goodnight, starting up the hill. Everyone seemed to be in their own homes, but he imagined some eyes peering out from behind the windows of these cottages, watching him and knowing where he was going. They whispered to each other, "He's going to the Sunny Corner..."
[44] The Sunny Corner. A name that was both chilling and fitting...
[45] As sharp as a snake's fangs...
[46] He quickly gathered his thoughts. He had to be sharp, decide what to say...
[47] 2
[48] Calgary walked to the end of this beautiful new road, lined with beautiful new houses, each with an eighth-acre garden. There were various rock plants, chrysanthemums, roses, lavender, and geraniums, with each owner showcasing their unique horticultural taste.
[49] At the end of the road was a gate with the Gothic-style Sunset Point inscription. He opened the gate and walked down a short driveway to the house, which was a well-built but unremarkable modern villa with a gable and a porch. It could stand in any upper-class suburb or emerging development. In Calgary's opinion, the house was worthless compared to its surroundings. The surroundings were truly magnificent. The river turned sharply at the cape, with towering peaks on both sides, lush and green; there was another bend upstream, with fields and orchards in the distance.
[50] Calgary looked at the river. He thought, "A castle should be built here, a castle that seems impossible to exist, only appearing in absurd fairy tales. A castle made of gingerbread or frosting." The house displayed elegance, restraint, and mediocrity, not lacking in money but lacking imagination.
[51] Of course, one cannot blame the Aigle family for this. They had only bought the house, after all, and it was not they who built it. However, it was still they or one of them (Mrs. Aigle?) who chose it…
[52] Calgary muttered to himself, "You can't put it off any longer…" and pressed the doorbell beside him.
[53] He stood there waiting. After a while, he pressed the bell again.
[54] He did not hear any footsteps inside, but the door suddenly opened without warning.
[55] Calgary was startled and took a step back. For someone whose imagination had been greatly stimulated, the scene in front of him was like a tragic goddess standing there blocking his way. A young face; it was a face that seemed to bear the bitterness of youth, with a tone of tragedy. He thought, "The tragic mask should always be young… alone, helpless, destined for misfortune… coming from the future…"
[56] He composed himself and let reason take over. She was an Irishwoman. Her eyes were a deep blue, with dark shadows around them, her hair was jet black and curly, and her head and cheekbones showed a kind of melancholy beauty…
[57] The girl stood there, young, alert, and hostile.
[58] She asked, "What do you want?"
[59] Calgary answered politely.
[60] "Is Mr. Ayrshire at home?"
[61] "He is. But he doesn't see visitors. I mean, he doesn't see people he doesn't know. He doesn't know you, right?"
[62] "No, he doesn't know me, but—"
[63] She began to close the door. "You'd better write a letter…"
[64] "I'm sorry, but I really want to see him. Are you… Miss Aigle?"
[65] She reluctantly admitted it.
[66] "Yes, I'm Hester Macquarrie. But my father doesn't see visitors without prior appointment. You should really write a letter."
[67] "I've come a long way…"
[68] However, she seemed unmoved.
[69] "Everyone says that. I thought it had all died down. You must be a reporter, I suppose."
[70] "No, not at all."
[71] She looked at him suspiciously, as if she didn't believe him.
[72] "Well, then what do you want?"
[73] Behind her, just a short distance away in the hall, Calgary saw another face. A flat, unremarkable face. If he had to describe it, he would say it was a pancake-like face, a middle-aged woman's face, with mousy brown hair sticking to her scalp. She looked like she was waiting there, a vigilant hag.
[74] "This is about your brother, Miss Aggle."
[75] Hester Aggle gasped, "Michael?"
[76] "No, it's your brother Jack."
[77] She shouted, "I knew it! I knew you were here for Jacqueline! Why can't you just let us live in peace? It's all over, it's finished! Why do you have to keep going on and on?"
[78] "You can never say something is truly over."
[79] "But this is over! Jacqueline is dead. Why can't you just leave him alone? Everything is over. If you weren't a journalist, I'd guess you're a doctor or a psychologist or something. Please leave. My father doesn't want to be disturbed, he's busy."
[80] She began to close the door. In a hurry, Calgary did what he should have done first, he took out the letter from his pocket and shoved it into her face.
[81] "I have a letter from Mr. Marshall."
[82] The girl was startled, she suspiciously held the envelope, hesitated and said, "From London... Mr. Marshall?"
[83] At this moment, the middle-aged woman who had been hiding in the hall suddenly joined in. She stared suspiciously at Calgary, making him think of foreign nuns. Of course, that was a nun's face! This face needed to be covered with a fresh, clean white headscarf or something like that, tightly wrapped around the face, and a black robe and veil. It was this face that, after much reluctance, allowed you to go in and brought you to the reception room or to see the Mother Superior before going through the thick door with the small window, where she would suspiciously look at you. She didn't seem like a contemplative nun, more like a servant in a convent.
[84] She asked, "Are you from Mr. Marshall?"
[85] The young girl looked down at the envelope in her hand and, without saying a word, ran upstairs.
[86] Calgary stood at the doorstep, enduring the suspicious and hostile gaze of the hag and the servant nun.
[87] He tried to think of something to say, but couldn't come up with anything. So, he wisely kept silent.
[88] Soon, the girl's cold and distant voice floated down from upstairs.
[89] "Dad said to let him up."
[90] Calgary watched as the watchdog reluctantly moved aside, but her suspicious expression remained unchanged. He walked past her, placing his hat on the chair, and climbed the stairs to where the girl was waiting for him.
[91] The interior gave him a sense of cleanliness, and he thought it could be a luxurious private sanatorium.
[92] The girl led him down a corridor, down three steps, and then opened a door abruptly, gesturing for him to enter. She followed him into the room and closed the door behind her.
[93] This was a study, and Calgary lifted his head with pleasure. The atmosphere in this room was very different from the rest of the house. It was a man's room where he worked and relaxed. The walls were lined with books, the chairs were large, although somewhat worn, but very comfortable. The papers on the desk and the scattered books on other tables were a bit messy, but not unpleasant. He caught a glimpse of a young woman leaving the room through a door at the other end. She was quite attractive. Then his attention was drawn to the man who rose to greet him, holding the opened letter in his hand.
[94] For Leo Aggles, Calgary's first impression was how thin he was, as if one could see right through him, almost not feeling his presence. He looked like a ghost! His voice was not very loud, but pleasant to hear.
[95] "Are you Dr. Calgary?" he said, "Please sit down."
[96] Calgary sat down and took a cigarette. His host sat down opposite him. Everything was done in a leisurely manner, and time seemed almost irrelevant here. Leo Agg's face broke into a barely noticeable smile as he spoke, tapping the letter with his pale fingers.
[97] "Mr. Marshall's letter said you have important news to tell us, but he didn't specify what it was about," he continued, his smile growing more pronounced, "Lawyers are always so careful not to implicate themselves, aren't they?"
[98] Calgary was surprised by the scene before him, as the man sitting across from him was a happy man. His happiness was not the usual lively, enthusiastic kind – it was a deep, hidden joy that satisfied him. He was a man who was not swayed by external factors and was content with what he had. Calgary didn't know why he felt this way, but he was surprised nonetheless.
[99] "It's good to see you," Calgary said, a very mechanical opening line, "I think it's better for me to come in person than to write a letter." He paused and then suddenly became anxious, saying, "This is difficult... very, very difficult..."
[100] "Take your time,"
[101] Leo Agher remained polite and distant.
[102] He leaned forward, clearly trying to help in a gentle manner.
[103] "Since you're here with Mr. Marshall's letter, I assume your purpose is related to my unfortunate son, Jack, whom we call Jacques.
[104] Calgary's carefully prepared speech was now lost. He sat there, thinking about the shocking fact he had to reveal, and began to stutter again.
[105] "This is too difficult..."
[106] There was a moment of silence, and then Leo spoke carefully: "If it helps to say it first - we already know that Jacques is… mentally unstable. What you have to tell us shouldn't come as a surprise. Despite this terrible tragedy, I still believe, one hundred percent, that Jacques should not be held responsible for his actions."
[107] "He shouldn't," said Hester, startling Calgary with her young voice, as he had forgotten her presence. She sat on the armrest of a chair behind him, leaning forward eagerly.
[108] "Jacques was always so annoying," she whispered. "He was like a little boy. I mean, when he got angry, he would pick up anything he could find and hit you…"
[109] "Hester, Hester… my dear," Agar's voice sounded pained.
[110] The girl gasped and covered her mouth with her hand. She blushed and suddenly seemed awkward, like a young person.
[111] "I'm sorry," she said. "I didn't mean - I forgot, I shouldn't say such things... especially after he - I mean, now it's all over, and..."
[112] "It's all over," said Aigle. "All of this is in the past. I've been trying... we've all been trying, to see this child as a patient. Something's wrong with his brain - I think that's the best way to put it." He looked at Calgary and asked, "Do you agree?"
[113] "No," said Calgary.
[114] There was a moment of silence. The blunt denial shocked his two listeners, and the word came out almost explosively. To mitigate the effect, Calgary said awkwardly, "I... I'm sorry. You see, you don't really understand."
[115] "Oh!" Aigle seemed to be thinking, and then he turned to his daughter and said, "Hester, I think you should leave."
[116] "I'm not going anywhere! I have to hear this, I have to know what's going on."
[117] "It might be a little uncomfortable…"
[118] But Hester impatiently called out, "What else has Jack done? What difference does it make? It's all in the past."
[119] Calgary immediately said, "Believe me, everything your brother has done is perfectly fine—in fact, the opposite."
[120] "I don't understand…"
[121] At that moment, the door at the other end of the room opened, and the young woman Calgary had just glimpsed returned to the room. She was now wearing her outerwear and holding a small briefcase.
[122] She turned to Aagel and said, "I'm leaving. Is there anything else?"
[123] Aagel hesitated for a moment (Calgary thought he always seemed indecisive), and then placed his hand on her arm, pulling her closer.
[124] "Sit down, Gwendolyn," he said. "This is—uh… Dr. Calgary. This is Miss Vaughn, she's… she's—" He paused again, as if he didn't know what to say. "She's been my secretary for the past few years." He added, "Dr. Calgary is here to tell us… or rather, to ask us some things. About Jacques—"
[125] "I'm here to tell you something," Calgary interrupted. "And, while you may not realize it, you've been making it difficult for me to say this, making it harder and harder to get the words out."
[126] They all looked at him in surprise. In Gwendraith's eyes, Calgary saw a fleeting glance of understanding, as if in that moment they had become allies. She said to him, "Yes, I know how difficult the Aggel family can be."
[127] Calgary thought to himself that she was a beautiful and charming woman - although not so young anymore, probably in her late thirties. She was plump, with dark hair and dark eyes, and exuded an aura of health and vitality. She seemed both competent and intelligent.
[128] Aggel said coldly, "I don't think we've been giving you any trouble, Dr. Calgary. It certainly wasn't our intention. If you could just get to the point -"
[129] "Yes, I understand. Please forgive me for my earlier words. You've been insisting - and your daughter - that everything is settled, over, and done with. But it's not. As someone once said, 'Any problem is not resolved until -'"
[130] "Until it's truly settled." Miss Vaughn finished his sentence. "Kipling said that." She nodded encouragingly at him, and Calgary felt grateful to her.
[131] "I'm about to get to the point," Calgary continued. "After you hear what I have to say, you'll understand my... my predicament. Along with my worries and concerns. First, I must say a few things about myself. I'm a geophysicist, and I recently joined the Heseltine Expedition to Antarctica, where I only just returned to England a few weeks ago."
[132] "Was it the Heseltine Expedition?" Gwendra asked.
[133] He turned gratefully to her.
[134] "Yes, it was the Heseltine Expedition. I'm telling you this to explain my background and to mention that I've been out of touch... for almost two years."
[135] She continued to smooth things over.
[136] "You mean, including things like murder trials?"
[137] "Yes, Miss Vaughan, that's exactly what I mean."
[138] He turned to Aghel.
[139] "If my words cause you pain, please forgive me, but I must double-check a few dates and times. On November 9th of last year, around six o'clock in the evening, your son, Jack Aghel—or Jacqueline, as you call him—came here to meet his mother, Mrs. Aghel."
[140] "My wife, yes."
[141] "He told her he was in trouble and needed money. Has this happened before?"
[142] "Many times." Leo sighed.
[143] "Mrs. Aigle refused. He became rude and threatening. Eventually, he stormed out, shouting that he would be back and that she 'better have the money ready.' He said, 'You don't want me to go to jail, do you?' And she answered, 'I'm starting to think that might be the best thing for you.'"
[144] Leo shifted uncomfortably.
[145] "My wife and I have discussed this matter at length. We...are very dissatisfied with this child. We've repeatedly bailed him out of trouble, hoping to give him a fresh start. In our opinion, perhaps a stint in prison would shock him...give him a wake-up call... He trailed off, "But please continue."
[146] Calgary continued, "Later that evening, your wife was found dead. She was struck down with a poker, which had your son's fingerprints on it, and a large sum of money that had been in her desk drawer earlier that day was missing. Your son was arrested in Dreamead, and the money was found on him, mostly in five-pound notes, with one bearing a name and an address that allowed the bank to confirm it was the same one they had paid Mrs. Aggles that morning. He was charged and tried."
[147] The fateful word was finally spoken—the word that held life and death in its grasp. Murder… It was not a word to be savored; rather, it was a word to be strangled, a word that was absorbed by curtains, books, and plush carpets… Words could be strangled, but actions could not…
[148] "From Mr. Marshall, the defense lawyer, I learned that your son claimed his innocence when he was arrested. Although not with great confidence, he seemed cheerful. The police placed the time of the murder between 7:00 and 7:30 p.m., and he insisted he had a perfect alibi. Jack Aggles said that during that time, he hitched a ride to Dreamead, catching a lift on the main road connecting Redmin and Dreamead around a mile away from here at about 6:55 p.m. He did not know the car's make or model—it was already dark—but it was a black or dark blue sedan, and the driver was a middle-aged man. The police made every effort to locate the car and the driver, but they found no evidence to corroborate his statement, and the lawyers were quite sure that the boy's story was hastily concocted and poorly executed…
[149] "During the trial, the defense argued that the evidence provided by the psychologists attempted to prove that Jack Aggles' mental state had been unstable. The judge was somewhat critical of this argument, which resulted in a summary that was unfavorable to the defendant. As a result, Jack Aggles was sentenced to life imprisonment. Six months into his sentence, he died of pneumonia."
[150] Calgary stopped, and three pairs of eyes fixed on him. Gwendolyn Wain showed interest and close attention, Hester's eyes remained skeptical, and Leo Aggles' eyes appeared blank.
[151] Calgary continued, "Can you confirm that all the facts I have stated are correct?"
[152] "Everything you said is correct," Leo said. "Although I still don't understand why it's necessary to go over these painful facts that we're trying to forget."
[153] "Please forgive me. I had to do this. I don't suppose you have any objections to the verdict?"
[154] "I admit that the facts are as you say—in other words, if you don't delve too deeply into the underlying issues. In essence, this is a murder case. But if you delve deeper, there are many things that could be said in his defense. The boy's mental state was unstable, but unfortunately, from a legal standpoint, this was not recognized. The McNodden Act [4] is narrow and unsatisfactory. I can assure you, Dr. Calgary, that Rachel herself—I mean my late wife—would likely be the first to understand and forgive the boy's thoughtless behavior. She was a very progressive humanist and well-versed in psychology. She would not have morally condemned him."
[155] "She knew how much Jake could be a pain," Hirst said. "He's always been that way—seemingly uncontrollable."
[156] "So, all of you," Calgary said calmly, "have no doubts at all? I mean, about his guilt, no doubt?"
[157] Hester's eyes widened.
[158] "How could we possibly doubt? He's certainly guilty."
[159] "Not truly guilty," Leo objected. "I don't like that word."
[160] "And, that word is incorrect," Calgary took a deep breath. "Jack Agha is... innocent!"
[161] * * *
[162] [1] The phrase "Crossing the Rubicon" refers to the act of making a decisive choice, committing oneself fully to a course of action. It comes from the story of Julius Caesar, who, in 49 BC, defied a ban and led his army across the Rubicon River into Italy, ultimately reaching Rome.
[163] [2] Pan is the Roman god, half-man and half-goat, who occasionally intervenes in human affairs.
[164] [3] "Jacko" is a nickname for Jack.
[165] [4] In 1843, a British citizen named MacNaghten mistakenly shot and killed the secretary of the then Prime Minister, believing him to be the Prime Minister himself. In the trial, the defense argued that he had a mental illness, and he was acquitted. In response to this incident, the British court established the "MacNaghten Rules," which set out guidelines for determining whether a defendant with a mental illness should be held criminally responsible for their actions.
[166] Chapter 2
[167] This statement was expected to cause a stir, but instead, it was met with a blank stare. Calgary had anticipated a mix of confusion, disbelief, and eager questions, but instead, there was only caution and skepticism. Gwendalyn Vance furrowed her brow, and Hester stared at him with wide eyes. Perhaps this was to be expected, as it was difficult to grasp such a statement all at once.
[168] Leo Aghael hesitated before saying, "Dr. Calgary, are you agreeing with my assessment? Do you also believe he shouldn't be held responsible for his actions?"
[169] "I mean, it wasn't him! Can't you understand what I'm saying, old chap? He didn't kill anyone. He couldn't have killed anyone. If the most unusual and unfortunate circumstances hadn't coincided, he could have proven his innocence. I could have proven his innocence too."
[170] "You?"
[171] "I was the one driving the car."
[172] Calgary's words were so straightforward that everyone took a moment to grasp them. Before they could react, the door opened, and the unremarkable woman strode in. She spoke directly and to the point.
[173] "I overheard it outside. This man says Jacques didn't kill Mrs. Aghael. Why does he say that? How does he know?"
[174] Her fierce, combative face seemed to wrinkle.
[175] "I have to hear it too," she said mournfully. "I can't stay out here in the dark."
[176] "Of course not, Mrs. Kirsten, you're family," Leo Aggles introduced her. "Dr. Calgary, Miss Lindström. Dr. Calgary is saying the most incredible things."
[177] Calgary was a bit confused by the Scottish name of Kirsten. Her English was good, but there was a slight foreign accent.
[178] She spoke to him in a reproachful tone.
[179] "You shouldn't be here telling these things, causing unnecessary trouble. They have already suffered enough, and now you come to bother them with this. What has happened is God's will."
[180] Her air of self-satisfaction and smugness made Calgary dislike her intensely. He thought perhaps she was the kind of person who enjoyed being cruel and gloating over others' misfortunes. Well, he would have to put her in her place.
[181] "At precisely 7:05 p.m., I was driving on the main road from Redmyn to Draymour when I picked up a young man who was thumbing a ride. I drove him to Draymour. We talked all the way. I thought he was a likable and charming young man."
[182] "Jake was very charming," Gwendolyn said, "everyone found him likable. It was his temper that got him into trouble. Of course, he wasn't entirely respectable.” She added thoughtfully, "But outsiders wouldn't have noticed that in such a short time."
[183] Miss Lindström turned her attention back to Kirsty.
[184] "He's already dead, you shouldn't say that."
[185] Leo Aggert became impatient. "Please continue, Dr. Calgary. But why didn't you speak up then?"
[186] "Exactly," Hester's voice sounded a bit out of breath. "Why did you run away? There were even ads in the papers…"
[187] "Hester, Hester," her father stopped her. "Dr. Calgary is still telling his story."
[188] "I understand your feelings very well," Calgary said directly to the girl. "I know what I feel… and what I will feel…" He continued, "Let me continue my story. It was quite late when I dropped off the young man whose name I couldn't even remember in the center of Dulmace. Since the police were quite certain that the crime took place between 7 and 7:30, I believe that this clears him of suspicion."
[189] "Yes," Hester said, "but you—"
[190] "Please listen to me. To make you understand, I have to go back a bit. I was staying at a friend's apartment in Dulmace, a naval officer who was out to sea. He also lent me his private garage car. On November 9th, I was supposed to return to London. But I decided to take the evening train and spend the afternoon visiting our dear old nanny, who lives about forty miles west of Dulmace in Pergas. I followed the plan. Although she was old and a bit confused, she recognized me and was happy to see me. In her own words, she was excited to read in the newspaper that I was 'going to the Antarctic.' I stayed there for a short time to not tire her out. On the way back, I decided not to go back to Dulmace along the coast, but to go north to Redmyn to see Canon Pisemash, whose study had some rare books, including an early work on navigation, which I very much wanted to copy. The old gentleman refused to install a telephone, considering it a device of the devil, along with radios, televisions, cinema organs, and jet planes. So I had to take my chances that he was at home. Unfortunately, the house was closed, and it was clear that he was out. I spent a little time in the cathedral and then drove back to Dulmace along the main road, completing the last leg of my triangular journey. I left myself plenty of time to go back to the apartment to get my bag, put the car back in the garage, and catch my train."
[191] "On the way, as I've already told you, I picked up a hitchhiker I didn't know. After dropping him off in the city center, I continued with my plans. When I arrived at the station, I still had some time, so I went out onto the street to buy cigarettes. As I crossed the street, a truck suddenly turned the corner at high speed, knocking me down.
[192] "According to eyewitnesses, I got up, obviously unharmed and acting normally. I said I was fine and had to catch a train, so I rushed back to the station. But when the train arrived at Paddington Station, I lost consciousness. Later, I was taken to the hospital in an ambulance, where I was diagnosed with a concussion. It was clear that this delayed effect was not uncommon.
[193] "A few days later, I regained consciousness, but I couldn't remember the accident or the fact that I was supposed to go to London. The last thing I remember is visiting the old nanny in Pembury. After that, there was a blank. They kept reassuring me that such things happen all the time. And the missing hours of my life seemed to have no importance. Neither I nor anyone else knew that I had driven through the road from Redding to Dulwich on the night of the murder.
[194] "By the time I left England, there were only a few days left. I was in the hospital, completely resting, and couldn't see any newspapers. I left directly to the airport and flew to Australia to meet the expedition. There were some doubts about whether I was fit to go, but I dismissed them. I was so busy with all the preparations for departure and in such a hurry that I didn't pay much attention to the murder reports. And by the time the suspect was arrested and the case went to trial, I was already on my way to Antarctica."
[195] Calgary paused for a moment. Everyone was listening intently to him.
[196] "I discovered this about a month ago, shortly after I returned to England. I wanted to wrap some specimens in old newspapers, so my landlady gave me a pile from her coal cellar. When I spread one out on the table, I saw a photo of a young man whose face was very familiar. I tried to remember where I had seen him and who he was, but I couldn't. However, it was strange that I remembered having a conversation with him about eels. His interest in the life of eels captivated him. But when was it, and where? I read the report, which said that the young man was Jack Agar, who was accused of murder. He told the police that a man in a black sedan car had picked him up.
[197] "Suddenly, all the memories that I had lost came back to me. It was me who picked up a young man who looked exactly like the one in the photo, took him to Duloe, dropped him off, and then went back to my apartment - and then walked across the street to buy cigarettes. I could only remember a little bit about the moment the truck hit me, and then I couldn't remember anything until I was in the hospital. I still couldn't remember how I got to the station to catch the train to London. I read the report over and over again. The trial had been over for over a year, and the case had almost been forgotten. 'A young man killed his mother.' My landlady could vaguely remember, 'I don't know what happened to him, but I think they hanged him.' I carefully went through the newspapers from that period and went to Marshall and Marshall Solicitors, who were the defense lawyers at the time. I found that I came too late to save the unfortunate boy. He had died of pneumonia in prison. Although justice could not be served for him, I believe that we can still do justice to his memory. I went with Mr. Marshall to the police station and presented the truth to the prosecutor. Mr. Marshall believed that he would present the facts to the Home Secretary.
[198] "Of course, you will also receive a complete report from him. But because I wanted to be the first to tell you the truth, he intentionally delayed it. I felt it was my responsibility and obligation to undergo this ordeal. I believe you will understand that I will always carry this deep sense of guilt. If I had been more careful when crossing the street...
[199] Gwendolyn Vane spoke up immediately, her voice warm and comforting.
[200] "We certainly won't blame you. It's... it's just unavoidable. Very tragic, unbelievable, but that's how it is."
[201] Hester said, "Do they believe you?"
[202] Calgary looked at her in surprise.
[203] "I mean, do the police believe you? How can you prove that it wasn't all made up?"
[204] Calgary couldn't help but smile slightly.
[205] "I am a reputable witness." He said mildly, "I testified without any personal bias, and they investigated my statements very thoroughly. Medical evidence, as well as various corroborating details obtained from Dulwich. That's just it. Of course, Mr. Marshall, like all lawyers, was very careful and cautious. He didn't want you to have too high expectations of success until he had a fair degree of certainty."
[206] Leo Aggul moved in his chair for the first time and spoke.
[207] "What do you mean by success?"
[208] "I'm sorry," Calgary said quickly. "The word is not appropriate here. Your son was accused of a crime he didn't commit, tried, and convicted, and ultimately died in prison. Justice came too late for him. However, this justice can be achieved and almost certainly will be, and everyone should know about it. The Home Secretary may advise the Queen to grant a pardon."
[209] Hester laughed.
[210] "Pardon? For something he didn't do?"
[211] "I know, these legal terms can seem impractical. But there is a convention, when a question is asked in the House, there is a clear answer, that Jack Agar was convicted of a crime he didn't commit, and the newspapers will report the facts without hindrance."
[212] He stopped. No one spoke. Calgary thought this must be a huge surprise for them. But all the same, it was good news.
[213] He stood up.
[214] "I'm afraid," he said hesitantly, "I have nothing more to say... to repeat how sorry I am, how terrible this has been, and to ask for your forgiveness... words that you must have heard many times. This tragedy ended his life and cast a shadow over my life. But at least... he spoke with an air of pleading, "let others know he didn't do that terrible thing... let his reputation, your reputation, be cleared in the eyes of the world... isn't that something?"
[215] If he had hoped for a response, he got none.
[216] Leo Agar slumped in his chair; Gwendraith's gaze remained fixed on Leo's face; Hester sat there, wide-eyed and despondently staring into the distance; Miss Lindström shook her head and muttered something under her breath.
[217] Calgary stood helplessly by the door, turning to look at them.
[218] It was Gwendraith Vane who took control of the situation. She walked up to Calgary, placing a hand on his arm, and whispered, "You'd better go now, Dr. Calgary. This news has shaken them too much, and they need time to accept it."
[219] He nodded and left. At the foot of the stairs, Miss Lindström caught up with him.
[220] "I'll take you out," she said.
[221] As the door closed behind him, he glanced back and saw Gwendraith Vaughan kneeling by Leo Aghel's chair. This surprised him.

[223] Her words were laced with disapproval.
[224] "His reputation must be cleared," said Arthur Calgary.
[225] "What lovely sentiments! But have you really thought about what this means? Men never think! I love them all. I came here in 1940 to help Mrs. Aghel run a wartime nursery for the children who were displaced by the bombing. She did everything for them. That was almost eighteen years ago. After she passed away, I stayed here to care for them, keep the house clean and comfortable, and make sure they had good meals. I love them all! Yes, I love them... and Jacques. He is a bad man, no doubt about it! But... he is a bad man!
[226] She whirled around, seeming to have forgotten that she had said she would take him out. Calhoun slowly descended the stairs. [229] "Why did you come? Oh, why did you come?"
[230] He looked helplessly at her.

[232] "Oh, justice!" She threw the word back at him.
[233] Calgary repeated, "I don't understand..."
[234] "Still talking about justice! What good is justice to Jake now? He's dead. What matters now is us!"
[235] "What do you mean?"
[236] "What matters is the innocent, not the guilty."

[238] "What matters is us. Can't you see what you've done to both of us?"
[239] He stared at her.

[242] "Oh...uh...Thank you."
[243] Dr. Calgary turned back to Hester, but she had retreated back into the house.
[244] The front door slammed shut.

[748] Dr. McMaster was an old man with a pair of bushy eyebrows, shrewd grey eyes, and a determined chin. He leaned back in his rickety armchair and studied his visitor closely. He found himself liking the man in front of him.

As soon as she has kittens, she becomes wildly protective of them, and if anyone comes near she will scratch them. But after a week or so, she starts to get back to her normal life. She goes out, catches some prey, and takes a break from her kittens. If anyone attacks them, she will still defend them, but she won't be constantly thinking about them. She'll play with them for a little while; and if they get too rowdy, she'll swat them and tell them she wants some peace and quiet. You see, she's getting back to her natural state. As the kittens grow, she becomes less and less concerned about them, and her attention turns more and more to the male cat nearby that she finds more attractive. This, I suppose, is what people usually refer to as a normal female lifestyle. I've seen many young girls and women with strong maternal instincts who just want to get married, but the main reason, I suspect, is that they desperately want to be mothers. But all of this will be balanced out. You see, from a purely physiological standpoint, the maternal instinct has been satisfied.
[769] "However, Mrs. Aggles' maternal instinct was too strong, and she had never experienced the physiological satisfaction of pregnancy and childbirth. Oh, the vegetables they ate before the age of five were strictly selected, the milk they drank was sterilized, and the water was tested. Because she really has laid it out for them, in a very good way. She wanted to give them a good home environment, a good education, ample pocket money, and a good start in their careers. She wanted to treat them like her own children with Leo Aggles. Of course, they're not her and Leo Aggles' biological children, they have different natures, emotions, talents, and needs. Young Mike is now a car salesman; Hester is almost like a runaway who became an actress, she fell in love with a very unattractive person, and her acting career was far from successful. Now she has to go back home and admit that her mother was right – she doesn't like to admit anything. Mary Dalton insisted on getting married during the war, her mother warned her not to marry that man, he was a smart and brave young man, but when it came to business, he was a complete fool. Then he contracted polio and was brought to the sunny coast as a recovering patient. Aggles Mrs. put pressure on them to stay there forever. Her husband was willing, but Mary Dalton was determined to have her own home. However, it's clear that if her mother hadn't died, she would have given in.
[787] "Mike, another young man, has always been a resentful young man; he hated his biological mother for abandoning him. He hated it from childhood and has never forgotten it. I think, at heart, he also hated his adoptive mother.
[788] "Then there's the Swedish masseuse. She didn't like Aggles Mrs., she liked the children and Leo. She received many benefits from Aggles Mrs., perhaps she tried to show some gratitude, but she couldn't. Of course, she had a certain aversion, but it didn't lead her to hit her benefactor's head with a poker. In the end, she could leave whenever she wanted. As for Leo Aggles…
[789] "What about him?" asked Calgary.
[790] "He's planning to remarry," said Dr. McMaster. "I wish him luck. It's a very good young woman. She's warm-hearted, friendly, and compatible with him. They've been together for a long time. What does she think of Aggles Mrs.? You can probably guess. Aggles Mrs.'s death made things much simpler. Leo Aggles is not the kind of man who can have an affair with his secretary while living under the same roof as his wife, and I don't think he would really leave his wife."
[791] Calgary slowly said: "I have met both of them, and I have talked to them. I really can't believe either of them... "
[792] "I understand," said Dr. MacMaster. "It's hard to believe, isn't it? But you should know that one of the family members did it."
[793] "You really think so?" asked Calgary.
[794] "I can't think of anything else," said Dr. MacMaster. "The police are quite certain that this case is not committed by an outsider, and the police may be right."
[795] "But who could it be?" asked Calgary.
[796] MacMaster shrugged. "I really don't know."
[797] "Do you have any idea about them, given your knowledge of their family?"
[798] "I can't tell you, even if I had an idea," said MacMaster. "After all, what would I base it on? In my eyes, none of them look like a murderer, unless I missed something. But… I can't rule any of them out. I can't. He paused and added, "My point is, we'll never know the truth. The police will do some investigation, they'll do their best, but after so long, it's hard to find evidence. And there were not many clues to begin with…" He shook his head, "No, I think the truth will never come out. Some cases are just like that. You can read about them in books. Fifty or a hundred years ago, some cases, you can be sure that it was one of three or four or five people, but there's not enough evidence, and no one can make a conclusion. "
[799] "Do you think this case is like that too?"
[800] "Well… hmm," said Dr. MacMaster, "Yes, I think so…" He looked at Calgary with a shrewd glance. "And that's the scary part, isn't it?" he said.
[801] "Scary," Calgary said. "Because they're innocent. That's what she told me."
[802] "Who said that to you?"
[803] "The girl, Hester. She said that what matters is the innocent ones. That's what you just said to me. We'll never know... Dr. McMaster interrupted him. "Who's innocent?"
[804] "Yeah, if we could only know the truth," he said. "Even if no one is arrested, tried, or convicted in the end. Just to know. Because otherwise... He hesitated.
[805] "What otherwise?" Calgary asked.
[806] "Think about it yourself," said Dr. MacMaster. "No, I don't need to say it out loud, you've already thought of it. It reminds me of the Bravo case, I guess a hundred years ago now, but there are still people writing books about it, using it as a perfect example, saying it was the wife, or Mrs. Cox, or Dr. Gleed. Even ignoring the coroner's opinion, insisting that Charles Bravo poisoned himself. All the theories seemed plausible — but now no one can ever know the truth. Florence Bravo was abandoned by her family, left alone to drink herself to death; Mrs. Cox, ostracized and with three young sons, lived a long life but most people who knew her believed she was a murderer; and Dr. Gleed's career and reputation were ruined —
[807] "Someone was guilty but got away with it. But the innocent ones, they couldn't escape."
[808] "This can't happen here," said Calgary. "Absolutely not!"

[899] 3
[900] At the social club in the East End of London, his favorite haunt...he had first met Rachel Constable. Her image remained vivid in his memory. A girl of medium height, sturdy, dressed in clothes he didn't recognize but which were exquisitely expensive, yet somehow shabby. She had a round face, earnest, warm-hearted, with a passion and innocence that touched him. There was so much to do, so much worth doing! She spoke with such enthusiasm, rambling on, although a bit incoherently, that his heart began to soften. For he too felt there was so much to do, so much worth doing; yet his nature had a certain irony that made him question whether the things worth doing would always succeed by right. But Rachel had no such doubts. If you did this, if you did that, if this or that institution received a donation, then good results would naturally follow.
[901] He now understood that she had never considered human nature. She always saw people as cases to be dealt with or problems to be solved. She never understood that everyone was different, with different reactions and traits. He remembered telling her not to expect too much. Although she denied it at the time, she had always had high expectations. She always expected too much, so she was always disappointed. He fell in love with her quickly and was pleasantly surprised to find that her parents were very wealthy.
[902] They planned their lives together, with ambitions rather than mundane concerns. But now he could see very clearly what had attracted him to her at the time, her passion. And that was also the tragedy, her passion was not for him. Yes, she loved him. But what she really wanted from him and from life was a child, and the child had not yet come.
[903] They consulted doctors, famous and unknown, even quacks, until she was forced to accept the conclusion that she would never have her own child. He felt sorry for her, very, very sorry, and willingly agreed to her suggestion of adopting a child. They had contacted the adoption agency, and during that time, they went to New York, where their car hit a child who had run out of a house in the slums.
[904] Rachel jumped out of the car and knelt by the child's side on the street. She was a pretty child, with blonde hair and blue eyes, with only a few scrapes. Rachel insisted on taking her to the hospital to make sure she was not injured. She went to talk to the child's relatives – the messy aunt and her obviously alcoholic husband – and it was clear that they had no affection for the child because she was an orphan and they had to take her in.
[905] "I can't take care of her properly here," she said.
[906] So Mary was brought back to their hotel suite. The child obviously liked the soft bed and luxurious bathroom. Rachel also bought her new clothes. In the end, the child said the sentence: "I don't want to go home. I want to stay with you."
[907] Rachel looked at him with a sudden longing and joy. Whenever they had the opportunity to be alone, she would say to him, "Let's keep her. It's easy to arrange. We'll adopt her, and she'll be our child. The woman will be delighted to get rid of her."
[908] He easily agreed. The child seemed quiet, well-behaved, and obedient. It was clear that she had no affection for her aunt and uncle who she lived with. If this made Rachel happy, he decided to do it. They consulted lawyers, signed the papers, and from that day on, Mary O'Shaughnessy became Mary Aigle, and they sailed back to Europe together. He thought that now the poor Rachel would be happy. She was indeed happy, excited, and almost ecstatic. She doted on Mary, buying her all sorts of expensive toys. Mary accepted them calmly and comfortably. However, there was something that made him uneasy. The child's easy acceptance and the lack of any form of longing for her homeland and family. He expected genuine emotions to be expressed later, but at that time, there was not even a hint of it. Accepting favors, being satisfied, and enjoying everything that others gave her. But did she love her adoptive mother? No, he couldn't tell.
[909] Leo thought that was when he deliberately retreated to the background of Rachel Aigle's life. She was a woman who was born to be a mother, not a wife. Now she had Mary, but her maternal instincts had not been satisfied. A child was not enough for her.
[910] From then on, her entire life was centered around the child. She put all her interest in orphanages, donation funds for disabled children, and children with intellectual disabilities, cerebral palsy, and those who needed orthopedic treatment - always children, truly admirable. He always thought that this was a very admirable thing, and it had become the center of her life. So gradually, he also immersed himself in his own affairs. He began to delve deeper into the economics and history that he was interested in, spending more and more time in the study, writing concise special articles. While his serious and happy wife was busy running the house, she also enriched her activities. He modestly and politely agreed and encouraged. "That plan is great, dear." "Yes, yes, I'll definitely support it." Occasionally, there would be a brief reminder. "I think you'll thoroughly investigate the situation before you get involved in it, won't you? Don't get carried away."<|
[911] Now she still consulted his opinion, but sometimes it was almost just going through the motions. With time, she became more and more like an autocrat. She knew what was right, she knew what was best. So he respectfully put aside his criticism and occasional advice.
[912] He thought Rachel didn't need help from him, didn't need love from him. She was busy, happy, and full of energy.
[913] Interestingly, behind the hurt he couldn't ignore, he felt a hint of pity. As if he knew the path she was pursuing was fraught with danger.
[914] In 1939, after the war broke out, Mrs. Aigle's activities immediately doubled. As soon as she formed the idea of opening a nursery for children in London's slums, she began to contact influential people in London. The Ministry of Health was more than willing to cooperate, and she had already begun to look for a site and ultimately found a suitable house. It was a newly built modern residence located in a remote area of England that was likely to avoid bombing. It could accommodate up to 18 children aged 2 to 7. They came from poor families as well as from unfortunate families. They were orphans or illegitimate children that their mothers did not want to bring with them or were tired of taking care of. They were abused or neglected at home, and there were three or four with disabilities. She also hired some household help for orthopedic treatment, a Swedish massage therapist, and two fully trained hospital nurses. The whole thing was not only based on comfort but also luxurious. Once, he advised her.
[915] "Don't you forget, Rachel, where we bring these children from, and they will have to go back there. You don't want to make it too difficult for them to go back."
[916] She replied enthusiastically, "There is nothing too good for these poor little guys. No!"
[917] He argued again, "Yes, but you must remember, they have to go back."
[918] However, she had already dismissed it. "Maybe not, maybe...we'll see."
[919] The war soon changed things. Nurses changed frequently because there were many real nursing jobs to be done, and they didn't want to take care of healthy children. Only an elderly nurse and Kirsten Lindström remained until the end. The household staff was also severely lacking, and Kirsten Lindström had to go over there to help. She worked tirelessly and selflessly.
[920] But Rachel Aigle remained busy and happy. Leo remembered that she occasionally had moments of confusion and doubt. One day, Rachel was greatly troubled by a boy named Micky, whose weight was dropping and he had poor appetite, so she called a doctor. The doctor found nothing wrong, but told Mrs. Aigle that the child might be homesick. But she immediately contradicted.
[921] "That's impossible! You don't know where he comes from. He was tortured and abused at home, and for him, it must have been like hell."
[922] "Well, that's true," said Dr. MacMaster, "but it's only natural for him to be homesick, and the important thing is to let him say it himself."
[923] And then one day, Mickey spoke up. He sobbed on the bed, pushing Rachel away with his fists and shouting: "I want to go home. I want to go home to my mom and our Oona."
[924] Rachel was very sad and almost couldn't believe her ears.
[925] "He won't want his mom, she doesn't care about him at all, and she beats him every time she gets drunk."
[926] He spoke softly, "But that's natural, Rachel. She's his mother, and he loves her."
[927] "She's not even a mother!"
[928] "He's her own flesh and blood, and no one can replace that."
[929] "But now, he should see me as his mother."
[930] Poor Rachel, thought Leo. Poor Rachel, buying all these things... not for herself, not for herself; she gives love, care, and a home to these unwanted children. She can buy them all these things, but she can't buy their love for her.
[931] Later, the war ended. Gradually, the children returned to London at the request of their parents or relatives, but not all of them. Some children were left unclaimed, and that was when Rachel said, "You know, Leo, now they are like our own children. It's time for us to have our own real family. Four or five of these children can stay with us, we can adopt them, raise them, and they will truly become our children."
[932] He felt a slight unease, but he couldn't quite put his finger on why. It wasn't that he disliked these children, but he instinctively felt the absurdity of the situation. The idea of building a family through artificial means was wrong.
[933] "Don't you think," he said, "that this is a bit risky?"
[934] But she answered, "Risky? So what? It's worth it."
[935] Yes, he felt it was worth doing, but not as confidently as she did. He had become so distant and detached, observing from his own little world, that it was no longer his place to voice objections. He had said what he had said many times before.
[936] "Do as you please, Rachel."
[937] She planned her scheme with enthusiasm and joy, consulting lawyers and handling matters in her usual organized manner. Thus, she obtained the family she had longed for. Mary, the eldest child, was brought back from New York; Mickey, the homesick boy, longed for his slum home and his neglectful and temperamental mother, and cried himself to sleep countless nights; Tina, a mixed-race girl with a deep complexion and elegant demeanor, whose mother was a prostitute and whose father was an East Indian sailor; Hester, an illegitimate child, whose mother was a young Irishwoman who wanted to start a new life; and Jackie, an interesting little boy with a monkey-like face, whose comical antics would make everyone laugh and who could always talk his way out of punishment with his wit, even earning extra candies from the strict Miss Lindström.
[938] Yes, Leo thought, undoubtedly, adopting these children and giving them a warm home, a loving couple, and care was a worthwhile thing to do. He felt that Rachel had the right to feel satisfied. But things didn't go as planned... because these children were not their own flesh and blood. Their bodies did not flow with the thrifty blood of Rachel's ancestors, nor did they possess the drive and ambition that her less well-known members used to survive in society. He dimly remembered the kindness and integrity of his father and grandparents, but he couldn't see any trace of it in these children. The wisdom radiating from his grandparents was also nowhere to be found.
[939] The outside world had done everything it could for them. These could provide great help, but they couldn't cover everything. They came to the orphanage because they had the seeds of imperfection, and under pressure, those seeds would germinate and bloom. This was well demonstrated by Jackie. Jackie, the quick-witted Jackie, with his lively witticisms, personal charm, and ability to manipulate everyone with ease, was essentially a juvenile delinquent. This characteristic had been reflected in his juvenile thievery and lying from an early age; all of this was attributed to his terrible upbringing. Rachel had said that these problems could be easily solved. But they never did.
[940] Jackie's academic performance was poor, and he was expelled from university. After that, there was a long string of painful events. He and Rachel tried their best to make the child believe in their love for him and their faith in him. They searched for suitable jobs for him where he could see hope for success if he put in the effort. Perhaps, Leo thought, they were too lenient with him. No, that wasn't it. For Jackie, he felt that no matter how lenient or strict the attitude, the end result would be the same. He wanted what he wanted and would get it, whether through legal means or not. He wasn't smart enough to successfully commit even minor crimes. So, when he ran out of money, he came back, angry and demanding money, threatening and threatening. Later, he left, shouting that he would come back, and that she had better have the money ready for him - otherwise!
[941] Later, Rachel died. All these memories seemed so distant to him. The long years of war between boys and girls. And himself? Also so distant and pale. It seemed that Rachel's abundant energy and passion for life had drained him, leaving him tired and powerless. Oh, how he needed warmth and love.
[942] Even now, he could hardly remember when he first realized that these two things were so close to him. So close... not given to him, but already there.
[943] Gwendolyn... a flawless and helpful secretary. She served him loyally, always ready to help, understanding and supportive. He had noticed something about her when he first met her that reminded him of the old Rachel. The same warmth, the same passion, the same eagerness, but only for him, not for the imaginary children she might one day have, but only for him. It was like putting a cold, stiff hand near the fire... a hand that had been empty for too long. When did he first realize that she liked him? It was hard to say, not like a sudden enlightenment. But suddenly... on a certain day... he knew he loved her.
[944] However, as long as Rachel was alive, they could never marry.

[946] Leo sighed, straightened in his chair, and took a sip of his now cold tea.
[947] Chapter Nine
[948] Just as Calgary left, Dr. McMaster welcomed his second visitor. This person was familiar to him, and he greeted him warmly.
[949] "Ah, Donald, it's good to see you. Come in and tell me what's on your mind. I can tell by the furrowed brow that you have something on your mind."
[950] Donald Craig smiled wryly at him. He was a handsome and serious young man who took both himself and his work seriously. The retired doctor liked his successor, but sometimes he wished Donald Craig could pick up on the humor.
[951] Craig declined the offer of a drink and got straight to the point.
[952] "I'm really worried, Mac," he said.
[953] "I hope it's not another vitamin deficiency," Dr. McMaster said with a smile. He found vitamin deficiencies to be a good joke. Once, a veterinarian had even pointed out to young Craig that a patient's cat had a severe case of ringworm.
[954] "It's not about a patient," Donald Craig said. "It's a personal matter."
[955] Dr. McMaster's expression changed instantly.
[956] "I'm sorry, my child. Very sorry. Did you get some bad news?"
[957] The young man shook his head.
[958] "No, it's not that. Listen, Mac, I need to talk to someone. You know all of them, you've been here for years, you know everyone, and I need to know too. I need to know my situation and what I'm facing."
[959] MacMaster's bushy eyebrows slowly raised.
[960] "Let me hear what the problem is," he said.

[1073] 2
[1074] Since becoming ill and losing his physical abilities, Philip Darwent had come to appreciate the value of mental activity. He had always been a very intelligent person, and now he realized that his intelligence could bring him resources and wealth. Sometimes he would amuse himself by predicting how the people around him would react to appropriate stimuli. His every word and action were not natural expressions, but deliberate, simply to observe their reactions. It was a game he played, and he would give himself a point when he got the expected reaction.
[1075] The result of this amusement was that he had, perhaps for the first time in his life, discovered his keen insight into the differences in human nature and reality.
[1076] In the past, human nature was not a subject of great interest to him. He either liked or disliked the people around him or met, finding some of them interesting and others boring. He was always a doer, not a thinker. He used a lot of imagination to come up with schemes to make money. The core parts of all these schemes were quite perfect, but a lack of managerial ability always led to their ultimate failure. So far, he had only considered people as pieces in a game. Now, deprived of his former lively life by illness, he was forced to begin to consider what kind of people they were.
[1077] At first, it was in the hospital that he had to pay attention to the emotional lives, hidden conflicts, and petty matters of the nurses. Well, he had nothing else to do. And now, this was becoming a habit. People - now they were all that life had left him. People. To study, understand, and summarize. He had to figure out why they did this or that, and then test his judgment. Honestly, it could be very interesting...
[1078] Only on this evening, sitting in the study, did he realize how little he knew about his wife's family. Who were they? Or, what were their inner selves, beyond the familiar exterior he knew so well?
[1079] It's strange how little you know about people, even if they are your wife.
[1080] He had pondered Mary while watching her. But how well did he really know Mary?
[1081] He loved her for her beautiful appearance and her calm, serious demeanor. Moreover, she was wealthy, which was important to him. If he had to marry a penniless girl, he would have to think twice. Everything was a perfect match, he married her, teased her, called her Polly, and enjoyed watching her puzzled expression when she didn't understand his jokes. But how well did he really know her? How well did he know her thoughts and feelings? Of course, he knew she loved him deeply. And just thinking about that love sent a slight shiver through his body. He shrugged his shoulders, as if to shake off a burden. It would be nice to escape from such love for nine to ten hours a day. It was good to realize this. But now he was surrounded by this love; he was cared for, cherished, and valued. This made him yearn for a little beneficial neglect... In fact, he had to find a way to escape. Mentally, emotionally - because there were no other ways. You had to hide in that airy castle.
[1082] Think about it. For example, who should be held responsible for his mother-in-law's death? He didn't like his mother-in-law, and she didn't like him. She didn't want Mary to marry him (did she ever think about who Mary should marry? He was skeptical). But she couldn't stop it. He and Mary started their happy independent life, and then things started to go wrong. First, there was the South American company, followed by the bicycle accessories company - both ideas were good, but there were serious financial misjudgments - then there was the Argentine railway strike, which turned everything into a disaster. It was just bad luck, but for some reason, he felt that Mrs. Aggles had some responsibility. She didn't want him to succeed. Then he fell ill. It seemed that the only solution was for them to move to Sunny Corner, where they were always welcome, and that could be guaranteed. He didn't mind that much. A disabled person, only half a person, where did it matter where he lived? - However, Mary cared.
[1083] Well, now they don't have to live in Sunny Corner forever. Mrs. Aggles was murdered. The trustee had increased the allowance for Mary within the trust, and they had moved out on their own again.
[1084] He didn't feel particularly sad about Mrs. Aggles' death. Of course, it would have been more comfortable if she had died of pneumonia or something like that in her own bed. Murder is a despicable act, and the sensational headlines it brings are repulsive. However, the murder itself was quite satisfying - the perpetrator clearly had some mental issues, which could be attributed to a whole bunch of psychological terms. Not her biological brother, but one of the "adopted" sons who often went astray with bad genetic traits. However, things don't look good now. Tomorrow, Detective Inspector Rush will come over with his refined English southwest accent and ask questions. Perhaps it's time to think about how to answer...

[1141] 3
[1142] Michael Ainsley lay in bed, gazing into the darkness, unable to sleep.
[1143] His thoughts were like a squirrel trapped in a cage, flipping through memories. Why couldn't he just let go of all that? Why did he have to carry the past with him all his life? After all, what difference did it make? Why did he cling to the musty yet pleasant room in the London slums, the nickname "Our Mikey," the carefree and exciting atmosphere, the fun on the streets, the camaraderie with other boys, his mother's bright blonde hair (which, with adult eyes, appeared to be the result of cheap hair dye), her sudden outbursts of rage when she turned around to scold him (of course, it was gin!), and her joy when she was in a good mood? Delicious dinners of fish and chips, and she would sing - all sad love songs. Sometimes they would go to the movies. Of course, there were always some "old boys" around - he had to call them that. His real father had left before he could even remember him… but if any of the old boys ever dared to lay a hand on him, his mother would not tolerate it. "Don't touch our Mikey," she would say.
[1144] Then came the excitement of war. Anticipating Hitler's bombers, the sudden air raid sirens, the whistling of shells. Hiding in the underground tunnels, spending the night there, how fun! The whole street was there, with sandwiches and soda. The trains ran almost all night. That's life, that's it! Exciting, never-ending!
[1145] And then he came here - to the countryside. A place where nothing ever happened!
[1146] "When all this is over, dear, you'll come back," his mother had said, so casually as if it wouldn't happen, not seeming to care about his departure. And why didn't she come with him? Many of the children on the street had left with their mothers, but she didn't want to go. She was going to work at a munitions factory in the north (with this "uncle," Harry Uncle!) to do her part for the war effort.
[1147] He knew then, despite her heartfelt farewell. She didn't really care… he thought. She only cared about gin – gin and those uncles…
[1148] And here he was, captured, like a prisoner, eating bland, unfamiliar food. It was unbelievable that, after a dinner of milk and biscuits (milk and biscuits!), it was only six o'clock and he had to go to bed. He couldn't sleep, so he cried. He buried his head under the blanket, crying for his mother, crying to go home.
[1149] This woman! She took him in, didn't want to let him go, spouted endless drivel, always making him play silly games, trying to get something from him, something he had no intention of giving. It didn't matter, he would wait. He was patient! Until one day, on a bright, sunny day, he would go home. Back to the streets, with the boys, the pretty red buses and the underground trains, the fish and chips, and the traffic and the cats of the area – his thoughts longingly wandering among all the things that made him happy. He had to wait, the war couldn't last forever. He was stuck in this godforsaken place, while bombs fell on London, half of it burning – oh! What a raging inferno it must have been, houses collapsing, people dying.
[1150] His mind conjured up horrific images.
[1151] It didn't matter, the war would end and he would be back with his mother. She would be surprised to see him grown up.
[1152] 4
[1153] Mickey Agha breathed a sigh of relief in the darkness.
[1154] The war was over, they had defeated Hitler and Mussolini... Some children had gone back. Time passed quickly... Then she came back from London, saying he would stay in the Sunshine Coast, become her little son...
[1155] He said, "Where is my mother? Was she killed by the bombs?"
[1156] If she had been killed by a bomb, well, that wouldn't be too bad. Many boys' mothers had been.
[1157] But Mrs. Airlie said "No," she wasn't killed. She had some very difficult work to do, and couldn't take good care of the children - something like that. Nice words, but meaningless... His mother didn't love him, didn't want him back - he had to stay here, forever...
[1158] Since then, he had been sneaking around, trying to overhear some conversations, and finally he did hear something, a few words from Mrs. Airlie and her husband's conversation. "Glad to get rid of him... Completely indifferent...". And a few words about a hundred pounds. So he understood - his mother sold him for a hundred pounds...
[1159] The shame, the pain, he could never overcome... She bought him! He vaguely saw her as a symbol of power, his insignificant power was useless in front of her. But he would grow up, one day he would become a man. Then he would kill her...
[1160] Once he made up his mind, he felt better.
[1161] Later, he went to school, and things weren't too bad. But he hated the holidays, because of her - she always made all the arrangements, made all the plans, gave him all sorts of gifts. She looked puzzled that he was so shy and reserved, not showing his feelings. He hated being kissed by her...
[1162] Later, he tried to find his biological mother, but found that she had died several years ago - in a car accident, she was with a man, the man was drunk and driving...
[1163] Why not forget all this? Why couldn't he just live happily? He didn't know why.
[1164] And now - what will happen now? She's dead, isn't she? To think that she bought him for a hundred pounds, that she could buy anything - houses and cars, and children, she didn't have any children of her own. She was the all-powerful God!
[1165] Well, she wasn't. Just one blow with a poker in her head, and she turned into a corpse, just like any other corpse! (Just like the blonde corpse in the accident on the North Road...)
[1166] She's dead, isn't she? So why worry?
[1167] What's wrong with him? Could it be that he can't hate her anymore now that she's dead?
[1168] So that's death...
[1169] Without hate, he felt lost... both disappointed and afraid.

[2276] "Let's go home, Philip, please. Let's go home."
[2277] "We will go home soon, not now. Things are still unfolding. I told you, Tina will come this afternoon."
[2278] "In what way?"
[2279] "Tina knows something."
[2280] "You mean... about the murder?"
[2281] "Yes."
[2282] "But how could she know? She wasn't even here that night."
[2283] "I'm suspicious. You know, I think she lied. It's quite interesting how some strange little things can turn out to be helpful. The maid who came to work that day, Mrs. Nalcoat – the tall one, she told me some news."
[2284] "What did she tell you?"
[2285] "Gossip from around the village. Some Mrs. Nalcoat's son – no, it's called Sir Cyril. He went to the police station with his mother because he saw something on the night Mrs. Aghel was killed."
[2286] "What did he see?"
[2287] "Well, Mrs. Narcot couldn't quite tell me, she hadn't gotten it out of the lady yet. But we can guess, can't we, Polly? Cyril wasn't in the house, so he must have seen something outside. That gives us two possible guesses. He either saw Mickey or he saw Tina. I guess Tina came here that night."
[2288] "She would have said."
[2289] "Maybe not. It's obvious that Tina knows something but hasn't said. Suppose she drove out that night, maybe she came into the house and found your mother dead."
[2290] "And then just walked away and said nothing? Nonsense!"
[2291] "Maybe there was a reason... She might have seen or heard something that made her think she knew who did it."
[2292] "She never liked Jacqueline, I'm sure she wouldn't want to protect her."
[2293] "Maybe she suspected someone else... but later, when Jacqueline was arrested, she realized her suspicions were wrong. But since she had already said she wasn't here that night, she had to stick to it. But now, of course, things are different."
[2294] "You're just making things up, Philip. You're spinning a bunch of nonsense that couldn't possibly be true."
[2295] "It very well could be. I'm going to try to get Tina to tell me what she knows."
[2296] "I don't believe she knows anything. Do you really think she knows who did it?"
[2297] "Not really. I think she either saw or heard something. I want to find out what it was."
[2298] "If Tina doesn't want to say anything, she won't say anything."
[2299] "Yes, I agree, she's very good at keeping secrets. Her face is always so stern, you can't tell anything from it. But she's not very good at lying. For example, she's not as good at lying as you... The method I'm going to use is guessing. I'll ask her questions and she'll just answer yes or no. And then you know what will happen? One of three things will happen. She'll say yes - and that's it. Or she'll say no - and since she's not good at lying, I'll know if what she says is true or not. Or she might refuse to answer, with that blank expression on her face - and that's the same as saying 'yes'. So what? You have to admit, this method has some hope."
[2300] "Oh, stop worrying about it, Philip! Really, stop worrying! Everything will blow over and be forgotten in time."
[2301] "No, this must be cleared up. Otherwise, we'll see Hester jump out the window, and Corston will go crazy. Leo is already as cold as a stalactite. As for the poor old Gwendra, she's going to get a job in Rhodesia [1]."
[2302] "What does it matter to them?"
[2303] "Doesn't matter to anyone but us – you mean that?"
[2304] Philip's face darkened with anger, which surprised Mary, as she had never seen her husband like this before.
[2305] She challenged him with a look.
[2306] "Why should I care what happens to them?"
[2307] "You've never cared, have you?"
[2308] "I don't understand what you mean."
[2309] Philip sighed irritably and pushed the breakfast tray aside.
[2310] "Take this away. I don't want it."
[2311] "But Philip—"
[2312] He made a dismissive gesture. Mary picked up the tray and left the room. Philip maneuvered his wheelchair to the desk, holding a pen and gazing out the window, feeling a strange mental oppression. He had been so excited just a moment ago, but now he felt unsettled.
[2313] However, he quickly regained his composure, filling two pages with writing in no time, then leaning back in his wheelchair to ponder.
[2314] It seemed reasonable and possible, but he couldn't quite be satisfied. Had he taken the right path? He couldn't be sure. Motivation. That was the missing factor. There must be something he had overlooked.
[2315] He sighed impatiently. Tina, he couldn't wait any longer. As long as this issue was clarified. Among the few of them. That was all they needed. Once they knew, they would all be free. From the suspicion and suffocating atmosphere. They could all go on with their lives, except for one. And he and Mary would go home, and then—
[2316] His wife... Whenever he thought of her, he seemed to see two people. One was the blonde, gentle girl he had married. That was the girl he loved, and he would tease her when she furrowed her brow and looked at him in confusion. That was his Polly. But there was another Mary - a steel-hard, passionate, yet unloving Mary, a Mary who cared only about herself and saw no one else as important, not even Philip, who was only important because he belonged to her.
[2317] A line of French poetry came to his mind - how did it go?
[2318] Amour, l'amour, l'amour!
[2319] And that Mary, he couldn't love. The cold, blue eyes behind her were those of a stranger - an unknown stranger.
[2320]
[2321] He chuckled at himself. He was becoming as tense as the others in the house, restless and agitated. He recalled his mother-in-law talking about his wife, about the cute blonde girl in New York who had wrapped her arms around Mrs. Aigle's neck and shouted, "I want to stay with you! I never want to leave you!"
[2322] That's love, isn't it? Yet, it didn't seem like Mary. Could such a drastic change occur from girlhood to womanhood? Could it be so difficult, almost impossible, for Mary to express her love and true feelings?
[2323] Just then – his thoughts came to a complete standstill. Could it really be that simple? No emotions, just calculations. Manipulations to achieve one's goals, feigned emotional displays. What else could Mary do to get what she wanted?
[2324] Almost anything, he thought – and the realization shocked him.
[2325] Philip angrily threw down his pen, spun his wheelchair away from the living room, and rolled into the adjacent bedroom. He slid up to the vanity, picked up a brush, and pulled his hair back from his forehead. He felt his face looked foreign to him.
[2326] Who am I? Where am I going? These were questions he had never had before... He maneuvered his wheelchair to the window and looked out. Down below, a day worker was standing outside the kitchen window, talking to someone inside the house. They spoke with a soft local accent, and their words floated up to his ears...
[2327] He widened his eyes, as if in a trance.
[2328] The sound from the adjoining room woke him up, and he maneuvered his wheelchair to the door connecting the two rooms.
[2329] Gwendolyn Wain stood by the desk, turning to look at him. The pallor on her face startled him in the early morning light.

[2331]"Hello, Philip. Leo thinks you might want to see The London Illustrated News."
[2332]"Oh, thank you."

[2334]"Quite the royal suite, wouldn't you say? Away from everyone, perfect for the disabled and newlyweds," Philip said.
[2335]He wished he hadn't said "newlyweds," but it was too late. Gwendolyn's face trembled.
[2336] "I have to get back to work." She said somewhat blankly.
[2337] "Perfect secretary."
[2338] "Now even that's not enough. I'll make mistakes."

[2340] "We may never get married."

[2525] The passage is from the Book of Proverbs in the Old Testament of the Bible, verse 28, which talks about a virtuous wife. The epitaph has been slightly altered from the original translation, which reads, "Her children rise up and call her blessed."
[2526] Chapter 23
[2527] 1
[2528] In the hotel room, Arthur Calhoun repeatedly reviewed his notes.
[2529] Occasionally, he would nod.
[2530] Yes... now he was on the right track. From the beginning, he had made a mistake by focusing too much on Mrs. Aggles. Nine out of ten cases taken this way were correct, but this case was the tenth one.
[2531] He had always felt that there was an unknown factor. Once he could isolate and confirm this factor, the case would be solved. To find this factor, he had been obsessing over the dead woman. However, now he realized that the dead woman was not so important. In a sense, anyone could have died.
[2532] He changed his perspective - back to the moment when all this began. He put his perspective back on Jacqueline.
[2533] Not only a young man unjustly convicted of a crime he did not commit - but fundamentally as a person Jacqueline. Was Jacqueline, as the old Calvinist doctrine says, "a ship destined to sink"? He had been given all the opportunities in life, hadn't he? After all, Dr. McMaster's opinion was that he was born to go astray, with no external environment to help him or save him. Is that true? Leo Aigle had spoken of him with indulgence and sympathy. How did he put it? "A person born out of harmony with everything around him." He had accepted the tenets of modern psychology. He was a patient, not a criminal. Hester said what? Directly, Jacqueline had always been so detestable!
